5.1 THE BASIC COMPONENTS OF A HEARING AID
5.1.1 Introduction
A hearing aid is simply a miniature public address system (Figure 5.1).
Sound waves strike a microphone, which converts this energy into a weak
electrical signal. The signal is fed into an amplifier. The amplified signal is
used to drive a loudspeaker, which converts the electrical signal back into
acoustic energy. The sound waves generated are then ‘piped’ into the
external ear canal. The acoustic energy delivered to the ear is always
greater than that received by the microphone. This additional energy is
provided by the battery.

The basic hearing aid system 
81
Hearing aids are signal processors in that they alter the input signal
with the aim of improving it for the user. Unfortunately it is not yet
possible to increase the signal strength without introducing some distor-
tion – that is, the output signal differs from the input signal in parameters
other than intensity. In some applications the signal is deliberately
modified with the aim of improving speech intelligibility.
The following sections describe the basic components used in a simple
hearing aid system.
5.1.2 The microphone
(a) Introduction
A transducer is a device that converts energy from one form to another
form. A microphone is a transducer. It converts acoustic energy into
electrical energy, either by direct contact with the vibrating source or,
more usually, by intercepting sound waves radiating from a source.
Microphones that pick up sound waves from the air have diaphragms
to collect the acoustic energy. They fall into two basic categories according
to whether one or both sides of the diaphragm are exposed to sound
waves.
Pressure microphones
These are microphones that have only one side of their diaphragm
exposed to the sound waves. The other side is sealed off, except for a small
aperture to allow the atmospheric pressure to equalize on both sides on a
long-term basis (like the Eustachian tube in the ear). These microphones
respond to minute variations in the surrounding air pressure. They there-
fore operate irrespective of the direction of the sound source – unless
their physical characteristics dictate otherwise – and are said to be omni-
directional.
Pressure gradient microphones
These are microphones that have both sides of their diaphragms exposed
to the sound waves. They operate by virtue of the difference in pressure
existing between the front and the back at any given moment. This differ-
ence is a function of the different path length for the sound waves
between the front and the back of the diaphragm. Obviously this path
length difference will vary with the angle of incidence; this type of micro-
phone has therefore a directional response.

82
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
(b) Types of microphones
There are four main types of microphone:
• The carbon microphone. In the carbon microphone sound pressure
variations, acting on a diaphragm, are applied to a collection of carbon
granules, thereby varying their resistance to an electrical current due to
an applied voltage. This type of microphone has a high output and a
non-linear characteristic, which tends to mute low level noise and
saturate when subjected to very loud sounds. It is thus very suitable for
use in telephones but, due to its high noise level and variable output, is
not often used for other purposes.
• The piezo-electric (crystal) microphone. The crystal microphone works
on the principle that certain crystalline materials such as quartz
generate small electrical signals when physically stressed. Various
configurations are produced in which the movement of a diaphragm
causes a wafer of the crystal to either twist or bend (Figure 5.2). Crystal
microphones have a very high impedance. They must be connected to
an amplifier (usually a field effect transistor – FET) with a matched
impedance otherwise the high frequency response will be lost. Low
frequency response is generally good and the very nature of the design
means that they are robust and stable.
Figure 5.2. A piezo-electric (crystal) microphone. (After Davis and Silverman, 1978.)
• The dynamic (moving coil) or electromagnetic microphone. The
dynamic microphone consists of a fine coil of wire around a balanced
armature, which is attached to a diaphragm and suspended in a perma-
nent magnetic field (Figure 5.3). When sound pressure waves hit the
diaphragm, the diaphragm moves the coil in proportion to the wave

The basic hearing aid system 
83
intensity and causes the coil to cut across the fixed lines of magnetic
flux supplied by the permanent magnet. This induces a current in the
coil, the amplitude of which is proportional to the distance moved and
the speed of movement. In order to reduce unwanted resonance
(vibration) of the diaphragm, the coil must be kept light, consequently
the impedance of moving coil microphones tends to be low. Stray
magnetic fields can cause interference.
Figure 5.3. A dynamic (moving coil/balanced armature) microphone (After Davis and
Silverman, 1978.)
• The capacitor (condenser) microphone. The capacitor microphone uses
electrostatic principles rather than the electromagnetic principles used
by a dynamic microphone. The microphone consists of two very thin
plates, one moveable and one fixed, which form a capacitor (formerly
called a condenser, hence the name condenser microphone). Variations
in the sound pressure cause the diaphragm to move with respect to the
back plate so that the capacitance varies in relation to the sound wave.
The capacitance is determined by the composition and the surface area
of the plates (which are fixed), the dielectric or substance between the
plates (which is air and fixed), and the distance between the plates
(which varies with sound pressure). In order to obtain an output, the
variable capacitance must be turned into an electromotive force (emf).
This is done by applying a polarizing potential through a high resis-
tance. Variations in capacitance caused by the movement of the
diaphragm are thus converted into a voltage across the resistor, which is
then applied to an amplifier (usually an FET). Some capacitor micro-

84
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
phones employ electret diaphragms (Figure 5.4). These are commonly
known as ‘electret microphones’. The term ‘electret’ describes a dielec-
tric substance that exhibits a continuing polarization after an electric
field is applied and then withdrawn. The electret polarization is analo-
gous to the magnetic retention of a permanent magnet. From the
viewpoint of a designer, a significant advantage of the electret principle
is that it obviates the need to supply the capacitor/diaphragm with a
polarizing voltage. Compared to the conventional capacitor micro-
phone, the electret offers a potentially higher signal-to-noise ratio, is
less susceptible to arcing and exhibits excellent frequency response
characteristics. However, the performance tends to deteriorate with
time. The electret microphone is the one most commonly used in
modern hearing aids. It is particularly suitable for miniaturization.
Figure 5.4. An electret microphone. (After Davis and Silverman, 1978.)
(c) Directional microphones
Directional microphones can improve speech recognition in noise by
improving the signal-to-noise ratio. Directional microphones may consist of: 
• One directional microphone, which has two sound entry ports
(Figure 5.5). Sound from the front is given prominence over that
from the rear by the inclusion of a time-delay network so that sound
from the rear is slightly delayed. If the delay is such that sound from
the rear enters both sound ports at the same time, the sound waves
will cancel each other out. Some low-frequency reduction is also built
into the microphone. The degree of extra benefit afforded is probably

The basic hearing aid system 
85
only about 3 dB or 4 dB in reverberant conditions (Madison and
Hawkins, 1983).
Figure 5.5. A directional microphone system.
• One directional and one omnidirectional microphone. This system
switches between the microphones so that only one microphone is
effective at any one time. 
• Two omnidirectional microphones. This dual microphone system
(Figure 5.6) replicates the directional microphone with a time delay to
the rear microphone. The directional pattern is determined by the
distance between the two microphones and by the internal time delay.
Variations in the time delay can produce different directional
responses. An adaptive digital microphone system is one in which the
directional response pattern is automatically altered so that the area of
Figure 5.6. A dual microphone system. (After Csermak, 2000)

5.1.3 The amplifier
(a) Introduction
Amplifiers are one of the most common of all electrical building blocks. By
definition an amplifier is any circuit that provides gain – that is a device
86
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
Figure 5.7. A polar plot illustrating a ‘cardioid’ directional response. A cardioid
response is one with maximum suppression to the rear (180˚).
minimum sensitivity is always directed at the most dominant noise
source in the rear plane. A single omnidirectional microphone is
preferable in some situations (for example in the street or listening to
music) and produces less circuit noise than a directional microphone.
One of the dual microphones can be switched off to give an omnidirec-
tional response.
• A microphone array, which can ‘beam’ into the sound giving an advan-
tage of several decibels.
A polar plot, Figure 5.7, is often used to illustrate the effect of a (single or
dual) directional microphone. A polar plot is a graph showing the output
of the hearing aid, whilst a sound source rotates from 0˚ to 360˚ around
the microphone of the aid. Sound arriving at the front, that is at 0˚, is used
as the reference point. 
degrees angle of rotation

The basic hearing aid system 
87
that delivers a higher energy output than the energy input. Of course,
amplifiers do not produce energy! The energy source for the output
power is an external one, which in the case of hearing aids is a battery.
Electronic amplifiers are not used with digital circuitry.
Hearing aids are complex pieces of equipment and contain many
electronic components. Among these components is the important semicon-
ductor device called the transistor. This device is an amplifier in itself, but it
needs to be connected to many other transistors and other components to
provide the sophisticated level of control and amplification demanded of a
hearing aid. Many transistors are housed in a single ‘integrated circuit’
component in modern electronics.
The bipolar junction transistor is an ‘active’ device that can be used to
control the flow of current through a load resistor by means of a small power
signal applied to its control terminal. One of the most common applications
of the transistor is as a voltage amplifier.
Modern amplifier circuitry often employs the use of a field effect transistor
(FET, also known as a ‘unipolar’ transistor). The FET is a semiconductor
device that depends, for its operation, on the control of a current by an
electric field. The FET acts as a pre-amplifier because it is able to amplify small
signals that are too weak for direct transmission to the amplifier.
(b) Amplifier classification and specification
In order to describe the requirements of an electronic amplifier for an appli-
cation it is necessary to specify certain parameters and its class of operation.
Amplifiers can be described according to:
• the electrical quantity they amplify;
• the frequency range over which they amplify;
• their relative input and output impedance;
• the class of operation.
The ‘class of operation’ is of particular interest and is therefore considered in
detail.
Class A
Class A operation of a power amplifying transistor is illustrated in Figure
5.8. With this class of operation the transistor is provided with a forward
bias so that a current flows without any signal present. The bias current
drain is related to the maximum current the receiver will handle. The bias,
or no signal, current exists even when there is no signal input and remains
independent of signal input level and of volume control setting.

88
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
Theoretically, an efficiency of 50% is possible. However, actual operating
efficiencies can be less than 25%.
When signals are present, the current drain fluctuates within certain
defined limits, but the average drain is still the same. Battery life is there-
fore fairly predictable. In the case of hearing aids, the fact that the power
from the battery is being constantly dissipated, even where there is no
input signal, is a major disadvantage.
Figure 5.8. Class A and class AB (class B) amplifiers.
Class B
A true class B amplifier is one in which the transistor is biased so that zero
current flows when no signal is present but there is considerable distortion.

The basic hearing aid system 
89
In high-power hearing aids, a modified version of the class B amplifier
is used. This modified version is correctly known as a class AB amplifier
(Duncan, 1988) but is more commonly referred to simply as class B. In a
class AB amplifier (see Figure 5.8) the transistor is provided with a small
amount of bias, just sufficient to allow the device to conduct slightly.
Compared to a true class B, there is no distortion; however there is a low
current drain when no signal is present. When a signal passes through, the
current drain depends on the required output level. The higher the
output required, the higher will be the current drain.
Two transistors can be connected in a push–pull circuit in order to
supply more power to the load than can be provided by a single-ended
amplifier (single-ended means that only one output transistor is used).
The output from the previous amplifier stages passes to a phase splitter,
which separates the positive and negative phases of the signal. The
positive phase is fed to one output transistor, the negative phase is fed to
the second output transistor. The positive and negative phases are ampli-
fied separately and then combined to deliver a full wave to the receiver. A
push–pull circuit is shown in Figure 5.9.
Figure 5.9. A push–pull amplifier.
The advantages of class AB push–pull amplifiers are:
• a greater power output is possible for a given transistor;
• the efficiency is greater;

90
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
• there is negligible power loss when the input signal is zero;
• because of circuit symmetry, distortion is reduced since it may be
shown that even numbered harmonics cancel out.
The current drain is proportional to the output signal. As a result, the gain
control setting directly affects battery life.
Class D
Classes A, B and AB amplifiers increase the input signal without signifi-
cantly altering its shape in the process. This is not so with the class D
amplifier. The input signal is transformed into pulses, the time duration of
which depends on the instantaneous amplitude of the signal.
The process begins with a triangular wave with a frequency that is at
least 10 times that of the input signal, typically 500 kHz. The input signal is
then added to this triangular wave. The difference between the two signals
emerges from a fast voltage comparator as a square wave with a duty cycle
proportional to the amplitude. This square wave is then used to switch
two output devices, which are either fully on or fully off, the difference
being that the times they are on and the times they are off are varied so
that the averaged effect can be made to replicate the input signal.
Class D amplifiers are very efficient, particularly at high gain, and can
produce something like a 50% increase in battery life. Class D amplifiers are
increasingly being used in more complex analogue hearing aid circuits. The
‘K-amp’ is not a class of amplifier but uses a class D output amplifier.
5.1.4 The receiver
(a) Introduction
In the hearing aid industry, the output transducer is usually called the
‘receiver’, whereas in the audio industry it is usually called the ‘loudspeaker’.
In principle, a receiver is a microphone in reverse. It converts the
amplified electrical signal into acoustic or sound energy and transmits it to
the ear. The receiver is the last and often the weakest link in the hearing
aid system. Problems stem from the need to produce a relatively wide
range of frequencies from such a small component.
(b) Air conduction receivers
The BTE and ITE receivers are generally of the balanced-armature type
(Figure 5.10). The alternating current output from the amplifier is fed to
the coil surrounding the armature, which causes it to move within the

The basic hearing aid system 
91
permanent magnetic field. This movement is transferred to the
diaphragm, which in turn generates sound pressure waves.
An external button receiver, as used with body-worn aids, operates on a
slightly different principle than that previously described. The signal
current in a coil, which is fixed to a core of permeable magnetic metal,
modulates the pull of a permanent magnet on a circular diaphragm made
of thin magnetic metal. The modulation causes the diaphragm to vibrate,
thus producing acoustic energy.
Figure 5.10. A balanced armature air conduction receiver. (After Katz, 1994.)
(c) Bone conduction receivers
Bone conduction receivers operate on the ‘reaction’ principle. A mass,
free to vibrate inside an enclosed case, is called to do so by a magnetic
driving system (Figure 5.11). The vibrations are transmitted through the
Figure 5.11. A bone conduction receiver (After Katz, 1994).

92
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
supporting spring to the case and then to the skin and the bony structure
of the head. Essentially, a bone conduction receiver is the same as an
external receiver, except that the diaphragm is fixed to the housing.
5.1.5 Client controls
(a) Gain (volume) control
Many hearing aids have a dial or serrated wheel that serves as a gain
control. It does not alter the input sound to the aid but adjusts the amount
of amplification of the input signal. The gain control is a potentiometer or
rheostat consisting of a fixed circular carbon track. A sliding contact arm
connected to the dial moves over the track and varies the resistance to the
input signal. An increase in resistance produces a decrease in amplifica-
tion.
The relationship between the movement of the wheel and the increase
in gain is not linear. Relatively little gain is available once the volume
control is beyond 50% of its total range. Most of the gain (approximately
70%) is delivered in the lower half of the control, while only a limited
amount is available in the top half. This is referred to as the ‘taper effect’.
Use of the upper half of the control may result in an unacceptably high
increase in harmonic distortion.
(b) ‘T’ switch
A hearing aid can be equipped with a special circuit, which makes it sensi-
tive to fluctuation in a given magnetic field. The circuit consists of a
magnetic induction pickup coil mounted inside the case. A fluctuating
magnetic field will generate a current in the coil, which is then used to
produce acoustic energy. The coil takes the place of the microphone on
the input component of the aid. If the ‘T’ position is chosen, the micro-
phone is cut out of the circuit. For further information see section 12.3.2.
5.2 ELEMENTARY ELECTRICITY AND ELECTRONICS
All matter can be reduced to its atomic structure. An atom consists of a
nucleus, which is positively charged, and orbiting electrons, which are
negatively charged. The nucleus takes its positive charge from its protons.
The number of protons is the same as the number of electrons, which
gives a state of electrical balance.
It is a basic principle that opposite charges attract and like charges
repel. In some materials, the force of attraction is strong and the electrons

The basic hearing aid system 
93
cannot be detached from the nucleus. These materials are insulators. In
other materials, the force of attraction is weak and the electrons are easily
detached. These materials are conductors. Easily detached electrons are
called free electrons. In order for free electrons to flow, an external force
or electrical pressure must be applied. An electrical current is a flow of
free electrons along a conductor.
If the current flows only in one direction, it is a direct current (DC),
such as a current from a battery. If the source of electrical pressure
constantly changes its polarity from negative to positive, the current flow
is alternating. Alternating current (AC) has a frequency; for example mains
electricity changes its polarity 50 times per second and its frequency is
therefore 50 Hz.
The direction and degree of AC current flow can be represented by a
sine wave. This indicates a smooth flow of energy, first one way and then
the other. This is the same representation as that used to show a pure tone
sound wave.
Current is measured in amperes (amps), although the small flow of
current found in hearing aids is measured in thousandths of an amp –
milliamperes. Electrical pressure is measured in volts. This is the force that
drives the current around the circuit. Voltage is really a measure of how
much energy is available to force the charge from one point to another.
Voltage applied to a conductor in a circuit produces a current. If there
were no other component in a circuit, the current flow would be
controlled by the electrical pressure. Some resistance is essential to limit
the current. Resistance can be likened to a tap that affects the mains water
pressure and restricts the flow of water.
A resistor, as its name suggests, resists or impedes the current flow. It
acts as a constriction and allows the current to flow less easily. Resistance
is measured directly in ohms, for which the Greek letter omega (Ω) is used
as the symbol.
The controlled passage of current through a resistance is expressed in
Ohm’s law, which states that the current (I) is directly proportional to the
voltage (V).
The ratio of V to I for a particular conductor is called the resistance (R).
This can be written as an equation: 
R = 
A triangle arrangement is useful in remembering the possible variants
(Figure 5.12).
V
I

94
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
A high resistance restricts the current to a low value; when resistance is
small, the current is large. Resistance is one type of impedance found in
DC circuits. Electrical impedance is the responsiveness, or lack of respon-
siveness, of an electrical circuit. In an AC circuit, impedance is termed
reactance. Capacitors are passive components used in hearing aids and
have the property that the greater the impedance, the higher the
frequency, as in a high-pass filter.
Transistors are active components, which work on the principle that a
small current flowing into a base, controls a much larger current flowing
into a collector.
5.3 SIGNAL PROCESSING
5.3.1 Introduction
Signal processing is a generic term that includes everything beyond simple
amplification (Wright, 1992). All hearing aids have some element of signal
processing. Many hearing aids provide automatic signal processing (ASP).
An electric current is more readily altered than an acoustic signal.
Sound is therefore transduced to electrical energy before it is processed in
some way. Many electronic options are available as user or dispenser
modifications in addition to the main hearing aid components.
5.3.2 Tone controls
Tone control is a process that provides either high (Figure 5.13) or low
frequency emphasis, and is similar to the treble (high frequency) and
Figure 5.12. A triangular aid to memory. (This triangle can be adapted for other
formulae such as those used to calculate battery life or wavelength.)

The basic hearing aid system 
95
bass (low frequency) adjustments on a stereo system. The tone control is
a filter network, usually between amplifier stages, which allows only the
desired frequency to pass through the system. If high frequency
emphasis is required, a high-pass filter (low frequencies attenuated) is
used, and conversely, for low frequency emphasis, a low-pass filter (high
frequencies attenuated) is employed. The terminology can be very
confusing, for example:
• low-pass filter = low frequency emphasis = treble cut = high cut = L.
• high-pass filter = high frequency emphasis = bass cut = low cut = H.
Tone controls can be either a switch or a potentiometer (variable
resistor), which is adjusted using a small screwdriver, or a computer
programmable control.
Figure 5.13. The effect of a bass cut potentiometer on the frequency response.
Reduction of the low frequencies provides a subjective high frequency emphasis.
5.3.3 Variable output limiting
All hearing aids have a maximum output, which cannot be exceeded
whatever the input level or the volume control setting. This level is often
restricted by the characteristics of the receiver.
If the maximum output is such as to cause discomfort to the user, some
form of output limiting should be used. There are two methods of
limiting: peak clipping and automatic gain control.

96
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
Peak clipping
Having been converted into electrical energy within the hearing aid, the
intensity of a sound wave can be thought of as being represented by its
amplitude. Peak clipping restricts the amplitude of the wave by cutting off
its peaks and hence reducing its intensity (Figure 5.14). Whenever the
peaks in the input signal exceed the pre-set maximum output limit, these
peaks will be clipped off. Peak clipping distorts the waveform by
producing something nearer to a square wave. This adds considerable
harmonic and intermodulation distortion (section 8.3.5). The distortion
produced by peak clipping has little effect on intelligibility because quiet
sounds, including the information-carrying consonant sounds (section
4.5), remain unaltered; only intense sounds are reduced, but this may
affect sound quality.
Figure 5.14. The effect of peak clipping and compression on a waveform.
Compressed wave

The basic hearing aid system 
97
In a class A amplifier, only the peaks on one side are clipped, which
produces odd and even harmonics. In a class AB (or ‘B’) amplifier,
clipping is symmetrical, producing only odd harmonics. The resulting
sound quality is generally more acceptable. Peak clipping should not
result in the aid constantly going into saturation; if this occurs the hearing
aid audiologist should consider automatic gain control.
Automatic gain control
Automatic gain control (AGC) is also called compression and, less
commonly automatic volume control (AVC). Automatic gain control limits
the output of the aid by reducing its gain or volume in the presence of
sounds above a pre-set limit (the ‘kneepoint’).
The use of compression in a hearing aid is generally dependent on the
user’s dynamic range, that is the difference or span between his or her
threshold of hearing and his or her uncomfortable loudness level. With
severe recruitment, a narrow dynamic range will mean that if peak
clipping alone is used to limit the output, weak sounds will not be heard,
intense sounds will be too loud and the aid will be in frequent saturation,
causing distortion and loss of information from the signal. 
The function of compression is to control the gain automatically as the
signal level changes. Up to a certain point, known as the kneepoint, the
gain increases linearly, that is a 1 dB increase in input results in a 1 dB
increase in output. Beyond this point, the gain is automatically reduced as
the sound level increases ‘compressing’ the signal and avoiding saturation
(Figure 5.15). Thus beyond the compression threshold or kneepoint, a
1 dB increase in input may result in, for example, a 0.5 dB increase in
output. This produces a compression ratio of 2:1, that is the ratio of input
change to output change. (For example, a compression ratio of 3:1 means
that an increase of 3 dB in input produces only a 1 dB increase in output.)
Compression aids may be obtained with different compression ratios or
where appropriate these may be digitally programmed.
Unlike peak clipping, compression has a finite onset and offset time,
known as the attack and release times. Attack time is the length of time
required for the controlling action to come into effect. Recovery or release
time is the time taken to return to normal. The attack time should be as
short as possible if it is to provide protection quickly at the onset of an
intense sound. The hearing aid should also have its maximum output set
such that it will provide protection against sudden intense sounds.
The release time has to be slower than the attack time to prevent the
aid from having an audible ‘flutter’ as the gain adjusts with the rapid
changes that occur in a speech signal. An attack time of 5 milliseconds and

98
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
a release time of 50 milliseconds could be typical for a hearing aid,
although there are wide variations and adaptive compression is available
with automatically varying release times.
Automatic gain control provides a feedback loop from a sampling
point. Below the preset limit or kneepoint the feedback is too small to
affect the circuit. When the kneepoint is reached the gain is automatically
adjusted. Automatic gain control may act in response to the input sound
level or the output sound level.
5.3.4 Types of compression
(a) Output compression
Output compression acts to control the gain of the aid in such a way that
the higher the signal intensity at the output stage of the amplifier, the
lower will be the gain. Output compression is the simplest type of
automatic gain control.
The kneepoint at which compression starts to operate depends upon
the level of the output signal (Figure 5.16). Reducing the kneepoint will
reduce the maximum output. However, since the signal level is monitored
after amplification, the maximum output is unaltered by the volume
Figure 5.15. An input–output graph for peak clipping and compression.
Input (dB SPL)
Output (dB SPL)
a

The basic hearing aid system 
99
control setting. Output compression is suitable for severe to profound
hearing losses as the volume control does not reduce the maximum
output. The kneepoint of compression is set at a high input level and
thereafter there is a high ratio (greater degree) of compression, usually 5:1
or greater. Clients with this level of hearing loss usually have a narrow
dynamic range and this type of compression gives them maximum (linear)
gain until the output is close to the uncomfortable level when compres-
sion cuts in to limit the output without the distortion of peak clipping.
Hearing aids with output compression cannot achieve normal loudness
growth but provide strong amplification with comfort.
(b) Input compression
Input compression differs in that the control threshold is part of the early
stages of the amplifier. It is therefore the input signal level that acts to reduce
the gain. The kneepoint is set on the input level and is independent of the
volume control setting. Whatever the volume setting the kneepoint remains
the same. The compression kneepoint does not affect the maximum output,
however the maximum output will be reduced with lower volume settings
(Figure 5.16). This is unlike output compression, where the maximum
output is unchanged by the volume setting. Input compression results in
better sound quality than output compression because it acts prior to ampli-
fication and therefore produces less distortion.
Figure 5.16. The position and effect of the volume control in input and output AGC.
Output (dB SPL)
Output (dB SPL)
Input 
(dB SPL)
Input 
(dB SPL)

100
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
Figure 5.17. Wide dynamic range compression seeks to correct abnormal loudness
growth by providing (a) more amplification to low levels of sound and (b) relatively
less amplification as the input sounds become louder.
(c) Special types of compression
Wide dynamic range compression
Wide dynamic range compression (WDRC) uses input compression with a
low kneepoint and a low compression ratio in order that the full range of
sounds is ‘shrunk’ to fit within the reduced dynamic range of the listener.
The hearing aid is in a ‘mild’ compression mode most of the time and weak
sounds are amplified much more than loud sounds. As with all compres-
sion aids, maximum (linear) gain is provided below the kneepoint. Because
the kneepoint is low in WDRC aids the compression control (sometimes
also called the TK or threshold kneepoint control) is viewed as a gain
booster for weak sounds. This means that weak sounds can be heard whilst
loud sounds are not uncomfortable for the listener. This is an attempt to
correct abnormal loudness growth (see Figure 5.17). Wide dynamic range
compression is ideal for mild to moderate hearing losses.
Level dependent frequency response
Level dependent frequency response (LDFR). These compression circuits
vary the frequency response with the input level. There are two basic LDFR
circuits: BILL (bass increases at low levels) and TILL (treble increases at low
levels). Some hearing aids use both BILL and TILL, for example using BILL in
the low frequency channels and TILL in the high frequency channels. 
Hearing level (dBHL)
Sensation level (dBHL)
Abnormal loudness growth
(sensorineural hearing loss)

The basic hearing aid system 
101
• BILL has a wide frequency response in quiet surroundings but activates
a low cut filter when the input reaches a certain level. This kind of
circuit is most suitable for flat or gently sloping losses and gives a high
frequency emphasis to high level signals which helps in background
noise.
• TILL is the opposite of the BILL circuit. It provides high frequency
emphasis at low input levels and a flat frequency response with high
input levels. This kind of circuit provides more gain for the high
frequency consonant sounds in speech than for the louder vowels,
which have less information content (see Chapter 4).
Frequency dependent compression
In frequency dependent compression the kneepoint or threshold is not
fixed but alters according to frequency. This means that compression is
only used in those frequency bands where it is necessary, rather than the
whole circuit going into compression. 
Curvilinear compression
Curvilinear compression has a compression ratio that increases as the
input increases, giving the characteristic curvilinear shape, see Figure
5.18. In effect, curvilinear compression combines wide range compression
Figure 5.18. An input–output graph showing curvilinear compression.
Input dB SPL
Output dB SPL

102
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
for low intensity sounds with compression limiting (avoiding saturation)
for high intensity sounds. 
The choice between the various modes of output limiting depends
upon the hearing loss and the client’s acoustic environment (section
9.2.2(d)).
5.3.5 Multi-channel systems
A multi-channel hearing aid facilitates the division of the frequency
response into sections or bands, which can then be treated differently to
provide a precise and complex frequency response curve that may be able
to match the client’s needs more closely. ‘Channel’ refers to a frequency
region that is processed independently. A ‘band’ refers to a frequency
region that is filtered. Where two channels meet, the processing will be
affected, which is known as ‘cross-channel coupling’. 
The need for a multi-channel system may be indicated by an unusual
audiogram shape, which would benefit from varying treatment in the
different frequency regions, as for example a steeply falling graph with a
region of good low frequency hearing. More than three channels in a
hearing aid may not necessarily be advantageous as, if the crossover
frequencies are too close together, the same speech sounds may be acted
upon in different ways causing confusion to the listener.
The different channels may offer not only the facility to alter the gain
but also the output and any compression in the various frequency bands.
Where each channel has its own compression circuit, different kneepoints,
compression ratios and attack and release times may be selected in the
different bands. Shorter attack and release times are usually preferred in
the higher frequencies than those used in the lower frequency channels.
The kneepoint can be altered in each frequency channel to suit the user’s
dynamic range. The frequency that marks each change in frequency
channel is known as the ‘crossover frequency’.
Twin channel aids may be relatively simple and their adjustment can be
effected using trimmer potentiometers. Multi-channel systems are usually
programmed via a computer, although such aids may be digitally program-
mable (analogue aids), most are fully digital.
5.3.6 Signal processing for noise reduction
Low frequency reduction 
A hearing aid with a frequency response that is well suited to a quiet
environment may be quite inadequate in situations of noise. Many hearing
aids therefore offer some switched in bass cut or ‘noise suppression’. This

The basic hearing aid system 
103
means that the aid amplifies the full frequency response in quiet condi-
tions but operates a reduction in low frequency gain when it is necessary
to reduce background noise. Some hearing aid systems provide automatic
noise reduction (ANR), so that the aid automatically adapts to the level of
background noise by reducing low frequency gain. When the background
noise ceases, the aid returns to its normal state. This kind of ANR is only
suited to clients who have a need for some low frequency gain; it cannot
work where there is no gain in the low frequencies to reduce.
Speech recognition
Certain ASP systems use the temporal as well as the frequency characteris-
tics to differentiate background noise. Background noise tends to produce
a fairly steady sound signal unlike speech, which fluctuates rapidly. The aid
may therefore reduce low frequency amplification in the presence of
steady state input signals, or may reduce all steady state signals regardless
of frequency.
Noise reduction programmes
Certain hearing aids offer a number of different frequency responses,
which can be selected by the user according to the acoustic environment.
The options may be selected using a switch or a remote control.
Responses are often programmed for:
• quiet conditions, where the full frequency response is available; 
• noisy conditions, where there will be some low frequency gain reduction;
• listening to music, where a full flat frequency response is provided and
any speech processing is inactive. 
Digital hearing aids are discussed further in Chapter 6. 
5.4 SUMMARY
A hearing aid consists of four basic parts:
• microphone;
• amplifier (or digital ‘chip’);
• receiver or bone conduction transducer;
• power supply.
A transducer is a device that converts energy from one form to another
form. In a hearing aid, the microphone and the receiver are the principle
transducers.

104
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
There are four main types of microphones: 
• carbon; 
• piezo-electric; 
• dynamic;
• capacitor. 
Some capacitor microphones employ electret diaphragms. ‘Electret’ is a
term that describes a substance with a permanent electrical charge.
Electret microphones are most widely used in hearing aids. The
diaphragm and the back plate of an electret microphone essentially form
the two plates of a capacitor separated by a small air space. The back plate
is coated with an electret film, which has a permanent charge. Variation in
sound pressure causes the diaphragm to move and thus varies the distance
between the two plates. The varying distance causes a fluctuating voltage
as an analogue of the acoustic signal. This is passed through a field effect
transistor (FET) for pre-amplification and then to the amplifier. The
electret microphone has a wide, flat frequency response, is accurate and
sensitive, has low internal noise and is easily miniaturized for use in
hearing aids.
A receiver transduces electrical energy into acoustic energy (or mechan-
ical vibration, in the case of a bone conduction aid), which is the reverse
function of the microphone. Internal air conduction receivers are generally
of the rocking or balanced armature type. External receivers, as for
bodyworn aids, are more usually of the ‘moving iron’ type. Bone conduc-
tion receivers are essentially the same as the moving iron air conduction
receiver, except that the metal diaphragm is directly connected to the
receiver case and therefore results in mechanical vibration.
Modifications to the hearing aid that may be available include tone
controls and output limiting. Multi-channel systems divide up the
frequency response into frequency bands, each of which is treated
separately. Automatic or adaptive signal processing aids automatically
adapt to the acoustic conditions in some way, this usually includes
reducing the low frequency gain in conditions of background noise. Input
compression is widely used in digital hearing aids. Where wide dynamic
range compression is used the aid is in a state of ‘mild’ compression most
of the time. This is most suited to mild and moderate hearing losses.
Output compression tends to be used for more severe and profound
losses. Output compression provides linear gain until near maximum
output but then goes into ‘strong’ compression to keep the output below
discomfort with the least possible distortion.

The basic hearing aid system

Chapter 6
Digital hearing aids
6.1 ANALOGUE AND DIGITAL
Since the introduction of digital hearing aids, traditional hearing aids have
become known as ‘analogue’ hearing aids in order to differentiate the
different type of signal processing involved. 
Acoustic energy is difficult to process directly. In an analogue hearing
aid the acoustic signal is converted or ‘transduced’ into an analogous
electrical signal, that is an electrical signal with the same waveform as the
original signal. The acoustic waveform and its analogue electrical
waveform are continuous signals. A digital waveform is not a continuous
signal but is made up of discrete (separate) values. Digits or numbers are
used to represent the electrical signal, not varying voltages as used in
analogue aids. In effect, a digital hearing aid uses a computer to process
the signal, which facilitates the use of mathematical techniques that would
be impossible with analogue electronics. The electric current passes
through the silicon chip of the computer as a series of pulses and these
form a very simple code, which consists of only two signals. These are
either off (no pulse) or on (a pulse). This is known as the binary code and
the signals are commonly written as 0 (off) and 1 (on). 
Amplification and other processing of the signal takes place before the
signal is converted back to an acoustic signal at the receiver. Signal
processing is a general term, which is generally taken to include every-
thing beyond straightforward amplification (Wright, 1992). All hearing
aids include an element of signal processing but in digital hearing aids the
signal processing is very sophisticated.
A conventional analogue hearing aid can offer adjustments using
resistor or ‘trimmer’ controls. However, the space required by the resis-
tors restricts the degree of flexibility. Digital signal processing (DSP) is
controlled within a tiny silicon ‘chip’ and can offer numerous strategies,
parameters and programs without compromising space. The increased

Digital hearing aids 
107
flexibility and accuracy offered by digital signal processing means that one
digital hearing aid fulfils the requirements previously met only with
several different analogue hearing aids. 
6.2 SAMPLING AND QUANTIZATION
6.2.1 Introduction
Digital signal processing involves converting an analogue signal into a
digital format. This occurs in an analogue to digital converter (A/D)
located at the beginning of the circuit, see Figure 6.1. The process consists
of two parts: 
• sampling (concerned with time); 
• quantization (concerned with amplitude).
When the signal has been converted or digitized, it can be processed by
predetermined instructions, which are referred to as algorithms. 
Figure 6.1. Block diagram of a digital hearing aid. (After diagram supplied by A&M
Hearing Ltd.)
6.2.2 Sampling
Sampling measures the signal at equally spaced moments of time. Any
amplitude values that occur between these spaces of time are not
measured and this information is lost. The sampling rate is important as
the signal must be sampled often enough to provide sufficient information
to recognize the original signal. The sampling frequency is the number of
Software
(alrorithm)
Analogue–
digital
converter
Digital–
analogue
converter

108
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
Figure 6.2. Although two sampling points are the minimum to allow us to recognize a
particular frequency component more sampling is required to represent the wave
shape. (After diagram supplied by A&M Hearing Ltd.)
times per second that the amplitude value of the signal is measured. Each
cycle of a wave must be represented by at least two sampling points (at the
peak and at the trough) to recognize the presence of a particular
frequency component. Many more samples are required to capture the
wave shape, see Figure 6.2.
The Nyquist frequency is the highest frequency component that can be
captured by a given sampling rate. For example, with a sampling rate of
2 kHz, the Nyquist frequency is 1 kHz. The minimum sampling rate must
be more than twice the Nyquist frequency (called after Harry Nyquist who
observed and published his sampling theorem in 1928 after work with
maximum transmission rates of telephone channels). 
A sampling rate that is too low will result in the points being joined up
in such a way that they represent a completely different frequency.
Transformation of sinusoids into different frequencies in this way is
referred to aliasing and obviously is not desirable, see Figure 6.3.
When considering complex sound made up of multiple frequencies it is
necessary to decide what bandwidth of frequency is required. Any frequen-
cies that are higher in frequency than the half sampling frequency will be
aliased (transformed into another frequency) causing error. An anti-aliasing

Digital hearing aids 
109
filter (low pass filter) is used to filter away signals of frequency above half the
sampling rate. Obviously the rate must be set so that important information
will be available and what is filtered away is of little consequence. 
In a compact disc recording, information is required up to 20 kHz so
the sampling rate must be higher than 40 kHz (twice the highest frequency
to be preserved: 2 × 20 kHz) and usually the rate chosen is 44.1 kHz.
In hearing instrument technology, where the bandwidth of speech
preservation is important, the sampling frequency rate is typically between
10 kHz and 20 kHz.
Figure 6.3. An aliased signal. (a) A very slow sampling rate is used. (b) Under-sampling
produces too few points to provide an accurate representation of the original signal.
(c) Errors arise when the points are joined together. (After diagram supplied by A&M
Hearing Ltd.)

110
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
6.2.3 Quantization
The purpose of quantization is to assign a recognizable mathematical
value to the amplitude of the sinusoidal wave at each point of time.
Whereas we usually count in base 10 (which involves using numbers 0 to
9), it would be difficult for a machine to work in a complex number base.
However, the binary (or base 2) system of counting uses only noughts and
ones. As it is easy for a machine to detect whether a signal is off or on, this
is used to represent 0 or 1. 
For example:
0 in base 10 is 0 in binary format
1 in base 10 is 1 in binary format
2 in base 10 is 10 in binary format
3 in base 10 is 11 in binary format 
4 in base 10 is 100 in binary format
5 in base 10 is 101 in binary format
6 in base 10 is 110 in binary format
7 in base 10 is 111 in binary format
8 in base 10 is 1000 in binary format.
Just as in base 10 we can create very complex numbers with a series of digits
between 0 and 9, so in base 2 very complex numbers can be created with a
series of noughts and ones. Figure 6.4 shows this graphically. The term ‘bit’
is used to refer to how many binary digits are available to represent a value.
The number of bits corresponds to the power of the number in base 2. For
example, three-bit processing would mean there were 23 (2 × 2 × 2 = 8)
quantization levels available. Ten-bit processing would mean there were 210
(1024) quantization levels available. The more levels there are available, the
more accurate will be the representation of the original signal. At present,
digital hearing aids use between 16-bit and 32-bit processing.
The flow of pulses is controlled by transistors or ‘gates’, which switch
the flow off and on. Thousands of gates are arranged in circuits to create
patterns of pulses that can carry out very complex calculations by working
through them step by step (Reffin-Smith and Watts, 1992).
A sound wave (sinusoidal signal) is converted into a series of levels that
correspond to specific amplitude values. The more levels there are, the
more amplitude values can be represented. Low quantization levels means
less accurate representation of the original signal as information is lost
and therefore errors can be observed in the quantized signal, see Figure
6.4. A high bit system will produce a highly quantized signal that can be

Digital hearing aids 
111
Figure 6.4. Quantization error (or quantization noise) reduces as the number of
quantization levels used increases. Each diagram shows different quantization levels
with the same sampling rate. If a signal is highly quantized, it can be viewed as a
continuous signal. (After diagram supplied by A&M Hearing Ltd.)

112
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
viewed as a continuous signal, although each amplitude measurement is
represented in binary format.
6.3 PROCESSING THE DIGITAL SIGNAL
As the information entered into the system exists as mathematical values
occurring at discrete time intervals, it is much easier to identify exact sections
of the signal. Decisions or instructions can be made based on the mathemat-
ical identification. Instructions referred to as processing ‘algorithms’ are
programmed into the chip and will dictate how the hearing aid treats the
incoming signals. Some of the instructions are ‘built in’ but many of the
processing strategies can be configured by the hearing aid audiologist to
meet the individual’s requirements. The hearing aid is programmed via a
personal computer (PC) using the industry standard ‘Hi-pro’ box as the inter-
face between the hearing instruments and the PC, see Figure 6.5. The
software database used as standard in the hearing aid industry is ‘NOAH’.
NOAH is the platform on to which hearing instrument software is loaded.
Each manufacturer has its own fitting software with associated ‘first fit’
algorithms, see Figure 6.6. After the entry of the audiogram the selected
hearing instruments are connected to the Hi-pro by the appropriate cables. It
is usual for the software to compute the setting of the hearing instrument as
governed by the fitting philosophy or algorithm being observed. Thereafter it
is the hearing aid audiologist’s role to evaluate the effectiveness of that first fit
and make any parameter adjustments required.
Figure 6.5. The ‘Hi-pro’ interface. ‘Hi-pro’ is short for ‘hearing instrument program-
ming’.(Photograph courtesy of A&M Hearing Ltd.)

Digital hearing aids 
113
Figure 6.6. Software showing the first fit response of a digital hearing aid using DSL
I/O targets. (Photograph courtesy of A&M Hearing Ltd.)
In DSP hearing aids, the input signal is filtered into distinct areas or
channels. Different processing can then take place in each defined
channel. The use of multi-channels is not particular to digital instruments
but the accuracy is greater than in analogue counterparts. Having discrete
areas or channels in which to operate provides the flexibility to reduce or
enhance specific parts of the signal. Although sometimes used inter-
changeably, the term:
• ‘channel’ refers to a frequency region where there is active automatic
signal processing;
• ‘band’ refers to a frequency region that is shaped by passive filters. 
A positive speech-to-noise ratio (to provide the optimum listening
environment for hearing impaired users) is partly achieved in DSP instru-
ments by noise reduction algorithms. Different strategies exist but most
noise reduction algorithms will identify the spectral frequency of noise

114
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
and that of speech in each channel. Where noise only is detected the gain
for that channel will be reduced. Multiple channels make it easier to act
independently on speech and noise. There is disagreement as to how
many channels are required to optimize listening. 
A variety of strategies are used in the different systems. These involve
combinations of some or all of the following:
• input compression (AGCi); 
• output compression (AGCo); 
• kneepoints;
• compression ratios; 
• attack/release timings; 
• cross-channel coupling; 
• feedback management systems;
• twin microphone systems;
• transient speech enhancement. 
Some of these features are available in analogue systems but the flexibility
and accuracy are much improved when used with DSP. Noise reduction
strategies aim to preserve the speech components comfortably within the
wearer’s dynamic range and to mimic normal cochlear function. There is
some disagreement regarding the number of AGC regions and the
attack/release times that should be employed. Other strategies may also be
used to enhance various speech components, for example by detecting
specific soft components of speech such as plosives and fricatives and
enhancing them with a short-term boost of gain.
Digital signal processing works to enhance signal quality and to
maximize speech intelligibility in noise. Processes can be accurately
applied to specified regions because DSP identifies exact frequency
domains in the same way as the grid reference on a map. However, DSP
strategies are of no help if the surrounding noise is speech (‘cocktail party’
noise). To combat this situation a dual microphone option (see section
5.1.2) is often used in DSP instruments. This provides the flexibility of
switching between omnidirectional and directional microphones. If users
can position themselves directly in front of their conversational partners,
use of directional microphones will give enhanced gain to their partner’s
speech even in situations of ‘cocktail party’ noise. 
After the signal has been processed it must be converted back to
analogue format before being sent to the receiver. This is essentially the
reverse of analogue-to-digital conversion:
• The processed signal is converted back to quantized levels by reversing
the effect of the sampler using the same sampling rate. The resultant

Digital hearing aids 
115
waveform will show steps, not because of the quantization levels,
which are fine enough not to be noticed, but because the signal was
digital and as such only exists at specified times not continuously as the
original analogue signal. 
• The steps are smoothed out. This is achieved using a low pass filter, set
to half the sampling rate such that only the original waveform content
remains.
6.4 FITTING PHILOSOPHIES
6.4.1 Introduction
The aim of fitting a hearing instrument is to compensate for the individual
hearing loss and to resolve the issues of handicap that arise as a conse-
quence. Despite the fact that the hearing loss itself can be described objec-
tively by measuring threshold, loudness sensation and uncomfortable
loudness levels (ULLs), the individual needs of the client cannot be easily
predicted. Fitting based on the description of the hearing loss can there-
fore only be the start of a fine-tuning process, which takes into considera-
tion the individual needs of the hearing-impaired person.
For the fitting of hearing instruments two different basic forms of
algorithms can be distinguished (see Table 6.1): 
• The prescriptive algorithms based on the mathematical calculation of
the hearing instrument settings from audiogram data. BERGER, POGO
and NAL (original) are the best known of this kind of algorithms.
These well-established fitting rules are not suitable to prescribe multi-
target gains for various input levels. They are based upon prescribing
gain for linear amplification on a basis of either a third or half
threshold.
• The adaptive algorithms take into consideration individual sound
perception of the instrument user. Most non-linear approaches are
based upon the premise of normalizing loudness. This means that the
prescribed gain for input sounds allows the hearing-impaired person
to perceive those input sounds at the same loudness as a normally
hearing person would perceive them. These approaches also attempt
to restore the normal perceived loudness relationships between
different frequency regions within speech. The hearing aid setting is
calculated from the difference between the individual sensation of the
hearing-impaired person and the statistical average of the hearing
sensation values of normally hearing persons. Examples of adaptive
algorithms include desired sensation level input/output (DSL I/O) and
National Acoustic Laboratory non-linear (NAL-NL1).

linear hearing instruments

6.4.2 DSL I/O
One of the most popular loudness normalization procedures is DSL I/O
(desired sensation level input/output). This is specifically designed to fit
non-linear hearing instruments, providing compression over the entire
dynamic range. In the case of multi-channel instruments it will provide
different compression strategies in each channel based upon the corre-
sponding differing dynamic ranges across the audiogram. 
The rationale of DSL I/O is to adjust the hearing instrument parameters
so that the acoustic dynamic range of a normally hearing person is
compressed into the residual dynamic range of the hearing impaired
person to be fitted. The objective is to make all sounds audible to the
hearing impaired user at the same loudness as they would be to the
normally hearing person.
The DSL I/O procedure requires threshold and uncomfortable
loudness level information at each frequency to determine residual
dynamic range and hence the compression strategy to be employed. The
maximum output of the amplifier is kept constant at the ULL so that ULL
values are not exceeded. If ULL values are not measured, they can be
predicted from the measured thresholds based on mean values from a
study group of hearing impaired subjects. In this case, the prescribed
targets are an approximation and adjustments may be required for the
individual fitting. 
The DSL I/O procedure is described as a level-dependent approach.
Three target curves are generated for fitting the hearing instrument. These
are for inputs of 40 dBSPL, 65 dBSPL and 90 dBSPL. Different values can
be determined within each channel. In the case of a single channel instru-
ment the values may be determined from 2 kHz or from average values
over several frequencies.
6.4.3 NAL-NL1
National Acoustic Laboratory non-linear (NAL-NL1) works on a different
premise from most other approaches. The methodology is comparable to
that employed for NAL-R and NAL-RP (see Table 6.1). The fundamental
objective of this approach is to provide gain to the speech frequency areas
such that it maximizes speech intelligibility but does not present speech
any louder than a normally hearing person would perceive it. It works on
ensuring that overall loudness is no greater than that perceived by a
normally hearing person listening to the same sound. 
Some methods, in their effort to restore normal loudness across all
frequencies, purposefully prescribe more gain for the lower frequencies as
these are perceived to be louder by the normally hearing person. However

Digital hearing aids 
119
this conflicts with what we understand to be better for speech intelligi-
bility, in that if low frequencies are amplified too much there is a chance
that these will mask the higher frequencies. NAL works to provide equal
loudness perception across all frequency regions. Some variation from
equal loudness is applied for severe to profound losses as research
suggests that this group of people use different cues for intelligibility
compared with lesser hearing losses.
A complex mathematical formula is used to give prescribed targets
based on input levels of 40 dBSPL, 65 dBSPL and 90 dBSPL.
The formula for NAL-NL1 is based on hearing thresholds, audiogram
slope and the level of the speech input signal. The way in which the
formula manipulates this data is based upon a total loudness calculation
and on the speech intelligibility or articulation index (see section 4.5). The
result of this formula is a prescription of gain at each of the frequencies
and speech levels.
6.4.4 Computer assisted ‘first fit’
Having selected the desired prescriptive approach, fitting systems will
automatically calculate the target gains. The hearing instrument’s parameters
will be programmed to comply with the input/output function prescribed. 
Most systems will give the option to enter other information, which can
be incorporated into the first fit algorithm. Details (such as whether the fit
is monaural or binaural, for adult or child, and, what tubing and venting is
to be used) can all be accounted for in the prescribed targets. Some
systems will provide for additional gain requirements if the hearing loss
entered is of a mixed or conductive origin.
First fit algorithms are based on objective research and mathematical
models. They provide a very good starting point for the setting of the
hearing instruments. The fitting must be verified and the aid fine-tuned to
take account of the client’s needs, experiences and satisfaction levels.
6.5 EVALUATION OF DSP INSTRUMENTS
Evaluating digital hearing instrument fittings may be achieved in the same
way as with any conventional hearing instruments, see Chapter 11, but the
stimuli used for testing digital hearing aids must be chosen with care as
traditional test signals may not be appropriate. The sweep or composite
noise signals routinely used in probe microphone, functional gain or
hearing instrument test box measurements are often detected by DSP
hearing instruments as noise. When these signals are used, the measured
performance of the hearing instrument will reflect the noise adaptive
parameters and not the gain supplied to speech. 

120
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
Most probe microphone systems offer ‘speech noise’ as a stimulus type
but this will also give rise to erroneous representation. Speech noise is
derived from the long-term average speech spectrum and is presented as a
composite speech noise signal, which is presented at a constant sound
level. Digital signal processing interprets this signal as noise because it is at
a constant level whereas the intensity of real speech fluctuates randomly.
Many digital hearing aids recognize the difference between speech and
noise by detecting the spectral shape (frequencies) and the modulation
characteristics. 
For this reason the working group HACTES (Hearing Aid Clinical Test
Environment Standardization) has developed a series of test stimuli for
the International Collegium of Rehabilitative Audiology (ICRA). These test
stimuli are referred to as ICRA noise signals.
They have been developed in an effort to establish standards for evalu-
ating the performance of hearing instruments in background noise and
their non-linear characteristics. The ICRA noise signals are based on live
English speech and are formatted to result in a long-term average speech
spectrum (LTASS) with the modulation characteristics of natural speech.
There are 10 ICRA noise signals including combinations of:
• male speaker; 
• female speaker;
• average speech levels; 
• raised speech levels;
• loud speech levels. 
Using these test signals provides information about how the DSP instru-
ment is responding to incoming speech levels. 
Some hearing aids provide the facility to switch off the adaptive
parameters of the hearing instrument so that gain can be directly
measured using any stimulus type. However, this will not demonstrate
how the DSP is working with background noise and various speech levels. 
6.6 SUMMARY
Digital signal processing offers increased flexibility due to a greater
number of filters (trimmer equivalents) giving:
• wide range of options; 
• accurate reproducibility; 
• calibrated alterations; 

Digital hearing aids 
121
• combinations of processing strategies; and 
• greater control. 
Digital processing can be used to produce a linear hearing aid but it is
most commonly used to provide non-linear compression to compensate
for cochlear hearing loss. Adaptive algorithms provide multi-target gains
(for soft, medium and loud input sounds) in order to restore normal
loudness perception. Thresholds (by air conduction) and ULLs (measured
or assumed from statistical data) are required for this. Loudness mapping
is sometimes also used to obtain more accurate individual data from the
client regarding the level of sound they perceive as soft, medium or loud.
The final result should improve acceptability by users as they are able to
hear more effectively in a greater number of situations. As with all hearing
aids, rehabilitation and after-fitting support are vital (see Chapter 12).
FURTHER READING
Johnson K (1997) Acoustic and Auditory Phonetics. Oxford: Blackwell, Chapter 2.
Rosen S, Howell P (1991) An Introduction to Digital Signals and Systems for Speech
and Hearing. New York: Academic Press.
Venema TH (1998) Compression for Clinicians. San Diego: Singular Publishing.
