14.2.2 Paediatric tests of hearing function
Pure tone audiometry is not appropriate for very young children and
different diagnostic tests may be used depending on the age and mental
ability of the child. The principle tests in use are:
• otoacoustic emissions; 
• brainstem evoked response (BSER) or auditory brainstem response
(ABR); 
• distraction tests; 
• co-operative tests; 
• performance tests; 
• free-field visual response audiometry (VRA); 
• pure tone audiometry.
Where ages are quoted these are intended only to provide a guide to the
child’s approximate stage of development.
14.2.3 Otoacoustic emissions
Otoacoustic emissions or ‘cochlear echoes’ are weak sounds produced by
outer hair cell vibration of the cochlea (see section 2.2.2). The outer hair
cells generate vibration that amplifies the peaks in the sound wave as it
travels along the basilar membrane. A tiny amount of the vibration is
‘reflected’ back out of the cochlea and through the middle ear to the ear
canal, where it can be measured as sound. 
Where there is an outer hair cell disorder, as is the case in most
sensorineural hearing loss, otoacoustic emissions (OAEs) or cochlear
echoes do not occur. Conductive hearing loss will also abolish the

Paediatric provision 
297
otoacoustic emissions because a healthy middle ear is required to
conduct them to the ear canal. The existence of otoacoustic emissions
therefore indicates normal or near normal middle ear and cochlear
function. Emissions can be absent even though hearing is normal but
where there are no otoacoustic emissions and tympanometry has been
used to rule out conductive loss, this is usually indicative of cochlear
damage.
Otoacoustic emissions are used in screening the hearing of newborn
babies. A probe containing a microphone and a miniature loudspeaker is
sealed in the ear canal, see Figure 14.3. The loudspeaker delivers a series
of clicks (brief broadband signals) and the microphone picks up the
evoked otoacoustic emissions. These are relayed to a computer for
analysis and display of the results. The presence of evoked otoacoustic
emissions (also called transient evoked otoacoustic emissions or TEOAEs)
is a strong indicator of hearing thresholds better than 25 dBHL. However,
otoacoustic emissions cannot be used to indicate hearing thresholds. The
test usually used to obtain some indication of threshold is auditory brain-
stem response (ABR) testing also called brainstem evoked response
(BSER) audiometry.
Figure 14.3. Otoacoustic emissions being used to screen a newborn baby.
(Photograph courtesy of Otodynamics.)

298
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
14.2.4 Auditory brainstem response (ABR) or 
brainstem evoked response (BSER) audiometry
Auditory brainstem response (ABR) or brainstem evoked response (BSER)
is one type of evoked response audiometry (ERA) that can be used with
young babies and other patients who are particularly difficult to test.
Electrical potentials from the auditory pathways, in response to sounds,
are recorded by electrodes attached to the baby’s head. All levels of the
sensorineural part of the auditory system, from the hair cells in the
cochlea to the cortex of the brain, respond electrically to sound. The
auditory responses are processed by a computer and displayed as a multi-
wave form. Changes in electrical potentials due to auditory activity are so
small that they must be ‘averaged’ over a period of time in order that they
can be singled out from the random activity of the brain.
Evoked response audiometry provides valuable objective results for a
population that could not provide this information in any other way.
Auditory brainstem response is the most widely used ERA for children. It
may also be used in some cases of suspected non-organic hearing loss
because no co-operation is required. The child has only to be still and may
be sedated if necessary. Brainstem evoked response audiometry provides a
good estimate of threshold, especially in the 2 kHz to 4 kHz region, and
should be interpreted in the context of other clinical information.
14.2.5 Distraction tests
Distraction tests are used both as a screening and a diagnostic test. They
are suited to children between the ages of approximately six to eighteen
months, that is from the time the baby can sit unsupported and turn its
head towards a sound, until it ceases to be sufficiently interested in the
sounds to continue to turn reliably.
Two testers work as a team, one positioned at the front to attract and
manipulate the child’s attention, the other stands behind the child and
presents the sound signals (Figure 14.4).
The object of the test is to record the quietest intensity at which the
baby responds to a range of frequency-specific sounds. The sounds must
be interesting to the baby and representative of low, mid and high
frequency ranges. Examples of those in common use include:
• low frequency – the voiced sound ‘oo-oo’ (approximately 500 Hz);
• mid frequency – a G-chime bar (approximately 1600 Hz);
• high frequency – a special high frequency rattle (above approximately
6.5 kHz) (Kettlety, 1987).

Paediatric provision 
299
Responses must be obtained at 30 dB(A) to pass a screening test but in the
diagnostic situation the intensity is raised until a response is obtained. The
intensity is measured using a sound level meter.
It is extremely important to be certain that the child is really
responding to the auditory signal and not to other clues. Some children
are very quick to respond to the touch of the tester’s breath, the sound of
a footfall, the smell of perfume or the movement of a shadow. These are
known, respectively, as tactile, auditory, olfactory or visual cues (Figure
14.5). Distraction tests appear very simple to the observer but they must
be meticulously performed to be reliable.
Figure 14.4. The position of two testers carrying out a distraction test. The baby sits
upright on the mother’s knee. Tester 1 manipulates the baby’s attention while tester 2
presents the sound signals.
Figure 14.5. A tester may obtain false positive responses during a distraction test if other
cues are present, such cues may be auditory, visual, tactile or olfactory. (After Anon).

300
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
14.2.6 Co-operative tests
Co-operative tests are used for children aged between about 18 months
and two-and-a-half years, when the baby has become too ‘grown up’ to
respond reliably to distraction techniques but is not yet able to perform
the next level of test. This age group is, contrary to the name of the test,
notoriously unco-operative. One tester is needed to carry out this simple
speech test, which provides only limited low frequency information and is
therefore used in conjunction with a brief high frequency distraction test.
The child is supplied with bricks or similar toys and the tester gives
simple instructions. There is a limited choice of instruction, such as ‘Give
it to Mummy’, ‘Give it to Daddy’, ‘Give it to teddy’, and the lowest inten-
sity at which the child can respond accurately is found. A child with
normal hearing should be able to achieve this at a level of 40–45 dBA.
14.2.7 Performance tests
Performance tests can be useful from the age of about two-and-a-half until
the child is able to undertake pure tone audiometry. The child is trained or
‘conditioned’ to perform an action, such as placing a man in a boat,
whenever he or she hears a ‘command’ sound, which for low frequencies is
‘go’ and for high frequencies is the sibilant ‘s’. The intensity of the command
is gradually reduced until the child’s hearing threshold is obtained. The
intensity is measured, as in all free-field tests, using a sound level meter.
14.2.8 Free-field visual response audiometry
Visual response audiometry, often known as VRA, is a technique that
requires more complex equipment than distraction tests but that can be
used for the whole age range, from about six months until the child can
perform pure tone audiometry. Loudspeakers are used to deliver frequency-
specific signals, in the form of warble tones or narrow-band noise. 
Young children do not find electronically produced tones very interesting
and some form of visual reinforcement is therefore used, often in the form
of a toy that lights up when the child responds correctly. If headphones are
not worn, the information obtained will relate to the better ear and, when
high intensifies are used, the signal can be distorted and no longer
frequency specific. Nevertheless, a limited audiogram may be obtained in
this way, usually providing points at 500 Hz, 1 kHz, (2 kHz) and 4 kHz.
14.2.9 Pure tone audiometry
Pure tone audiometry should be used as soon as the child is able to co-
operate reliably since the ears can be tested separately. Many children

Paediatric provision 
301
from around the age of three can be trained to carry out a pure tone test if
it is treated as a game. ‘Play techniques’ are used in which the child is
taught to respond to the tones by performing some action, such as placing
a peg in a board.
A restricted range of frequencies is often tested, such as 500 Hz, 1 kHz,
(2 kHz) and 4 kHz, because the child may lose concentration before a full
test can be completed. Masking is also usually possible with children from
about five years old, if the instructions are simplified and the ‘play-like’
nature of the test is retained.
14.3 HABILITATION OF HEARING-IMPAIRED
CHILDREN
14.3.1 The habilitation process
A multidisciplinary team is usually involved with the diagnosis and
management of the hearing-impaired child. Such a team may include:
• audiological physician or otologist;
• clinical or educational audiologist;
• teacher of the deaf;
• educational psychologist;
• social worker;
• other specialists.
In the initial stages, parents have to come to terms with the child’s impair-
ment and it is usually the audiological physician or otologist who counsels
the parents and who will answer any medical questions they may have.
Parents may feel a sense of guilt, numbness, fear and isolation, feelings
with which they have to come to terms before they can help their child
(see section 12.1.4). After the initial diagnosis, support and counselling
will be undertaken by a peripatetic teacher of the deaf, who will see the
family on a regular basis, in the home and later in the school. The
counselling needed to support parents is of great importance.
The selection of appropriate hearing aids is central to the habilitation
process in order to provide maximum use of residual hearing. The funda-
mental principles of hearing aid selection are the same for children as for
adults but provision should be made as early as possible, with binaural
fitting considered as the norm. It is also important that hearing aids are
worn constantly from the beginning and that they are checked regularly by
an adult to ensure they are working. Hearing aids must therefore be
manageable for the family and child.

302
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
Hearing aids for children usually need repairing at least once a year and
replacing every three to four years (British Association of Audiological
Physicians, 1990). In addition, new earmoulds are required several times a
year and batteries need frequent replacement.
There are three main types of personal hearing aid systems in use with
hearing-impaired children at present: bodyworn, postaural and in-the-ear
aids. The most widely used system is currently the postaural hearing aid.
In-the-ear hearing aids are increasing in popularity but these have to be
replaced or re-shelled at frequent intervals as the child grows.
Where audiological and other factors allow for choice of the hearing aid
system, parental feelings should be taken into consideration. If the
parents of the child are not happy with the hearing aid system, the child is
unlikely to make the best use of it. Many children are provided with
commercial hearing aids under the National Health Service when it is felt
there is a need that cannot be met by the standard range. 
Many deaf children are provided with radio hearing aids (see section
14.4.2) in addition to their personal aids. These overcome some of the
problems of interference from background noise.
14.3.2 Fitting hearing aids to very young children
The choice of hearing aids for very young children is difficult because
audiometric information will be limited. The hearing aids must therefore
be very flexible and the fitting must not be seen as being final. Frequent
review appointments will be required in the early stages.
The size of a small child’s ear canals has to be taken into account in the
fitting, because there will be important differences from average adult ear
canal data. A baby’s ear canal could have, for example, a volume of 0.5 cc
whereas an ear simulator has a volume of 2 cc. The smaller ear canal
volume will lead to a significant increase in sound pressure over that in the
ear simulator. Insertion gain measurements using a probe tube micro-
phone (see section 11.2) are very helpful in quantifying the baby’s real ear
gain. The DSL (desired sensation level) prescription formula (Seewald,
1994 and 2000) is often used with children as it includes predicted values
for children of varying age. 
Behind-the-ear or postaural hearing aids are routinely fitted to
children. Where these are to be fitted to small children and babies, minia-
ture postaural hearing aids should be used. Specific hearing aid features
that may be desirable include:
• direct audio input (for radio hearing aids);
• M-T switch (for hearing own voice as well as telecoil or audio input);
• locking battery drawer;
• locking volume control or automatic digital volume control.

Paediatric provision 
303
Difficulties of fitting include creating satisfactory earmoulds and retention
of the aids on tiny ears.
Earmoulds should be made in a soft material and may need replace-
ment as often as every 10 to 14 days in the early months when the baby is
growing quickly. Impressions can be taken whilst the baby is asleep or
being fed, as at this time the baby will be relatively still. Impressions
should be taken slowly and carefully, using an addition cured silicone
material (see section 10.1.2). Older children enjoy playing with the excess
impression material; the task of making hedgehogs, worms and balls
usually very successfully keeps them occupied while the impressions are
setting. If children are not used to having impressions taken, it may help
for them to watch first while the audiologist takes or pretends to take an
impression of Mummy’s ear or even of a teddy bear’s or doll’s ear.
Retention of the hearing aids on small ears can be facilitated using
plastic rings or ‘huggies’ to which the hearing aids are attached – see
Figure 14.6. The ring is fitted around the ear. Double-sided toupee tape is
sometimes used to stick the aids to the head but this is not really suitable
as a baby’s skin is very delicate.
Figure 14.6. (a) Hearing aid retention rings. (b) With the aid attached. (c) Placed on
the child’s ear.
(a)
(b)
(c)

304
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
14.3.3 Children with complex needs
The National Deaf Children’s Society (NDCS, 1996) suggests that up to
49% of children born with permanent bilateral hearing loss of moderate or
greater degree will have additional needs such as visual problems or devel-
opmental delay. Most of these children can be assessed by modifying
normal audiometric procedures. This could, for example, involve using
play audiometry techniques at an older than normal age or accepting
unusual responses. A response may be any change in the pre-stimulus
state of behaviour. Extra and novel reinforcement of correct responses are
often required to maintain the child’s attention, for example, using visual
reinforcers such as colourful toys and puppets or flashing lights. It is also
important to include ‘no sound’ trials to take account of any checking
behaviour from the child.
Tympanometry can be used to screen for middle ear problems and
evoked response audiometry may be used where other forms of threshold
testing are not possible. Sedation may be necessary and the results have to
be interpreted cautiously as they may be influenced by sleep and sedation
and also by neurological factors.
Fitting and managing hearing aids may also call for creative thinking.
Amplification must be seen in context and hearing aids must meet the
needs of both the child and of his or her family.
14.3.4 Cochlear implants
Cochlear implants are hearing aids that electrically stimulate the auditory
nerve to produce a sensation of sound. Implantation involves a surgical
procedure in which an electrode array is implanted into the cochlea.
Different speech processing strategies are used in the various cochlear
implants available, for example, the whole speech signal may be delivered
or specific features of speech may be extracted. The components of the
cochlear implant system are shown in Figure 14.7. The microphone of the
unit is carried at ear level but the sound processor may be postaural or
bodyworn. 
Following the operation and time for healing, the external parts of 
the implant system are attached and programmed. The programme is 
fine-tuned over several visits. The programme is often referred to as a
‘map’. This is really an electrical audiogram that shows electrical thresh-
olds (T) and electrical comfort levels (C) for each electrode. The T and C
levels may appear to change in the first few months after ‘switch-on’ as the
child becomes accustomed to the signal and to the testing routine. When
changes are made to the map these may alter the perceived sound quality
of the implant signal. Young children are not able to give appropriate

Paediatric provision 
305
feedback and, for these children, changes to the map will be based on
behavioural observations made by parents and teachers. Sound field
thresholds may also used to monitor the appropriateness of the map.
Children who are implanted at an early age may develop speech and
language in the same way as normal infants without active intervention.
Older children and adults will need ongoing rehabilitation directed
towards developing listening skills and spoken language.
Each implant centre has guidelines as to who may benefit from implan-
tation. Guidelines are continually being revised but currently (Archbold,
2000) children are being considered whose aided responses to sound field
audiometry are worse than 55 dB(A) at 2 kHz and 4 kHz. Most centres
require that children have worn hearing aids with well-fitting earmoulds
for at least several months and that they show little functional benefit from
their aids. Children with additional needs are considered by many but not
all centres. Good educational support and a supportive motivated family
with realistic expectations are both factors seen as likely to affect the long-
term outcome. It is recognized that children born with profound hearing
Figure 14.7. Components of a cochlear implant. (After Maltby and Knight, 2000.)
Transmitter
Microphone
Leads
Speech
processor
Magnet
Decoder
Electrodes
External parts                          Implanted parts

306
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
loss need to receive an implant early and many centres implant children at
less than two years old. Some older children are being implanted but
these are generally children who were born hearing and then became
profoundly deaf following an accident or illness such as meningitis. It is
now evident that the majority of profoundly deaf children will hear more
with a cochlear implant than with hearing aids and some centres are
considering implants for severely deaf people.
14.4 CLASSROOM LISTENING
14.4.1 Classroom conditions
The classroom presents a complex listening environment for children.
They are expected to pay attention to the teacher’s speech and ignore
irrelevant conversations and instructions, often in noisy conditions with
several different activities going on simultaneously. The ‘soundscape’
(listening environment) is constantly changing. The signal-to-noise ratio
changes as activities go from quiet to noisy and reverberation times vary as
children move from classroom to, for example, hall or gymnasium. The
signal children receive in the classroom may often be distorted by long
reverberation times and masked by background noise. Children who have
a hearing loss will have difficulty in interpreting speech in these difficult
conditions. Even children with mild hearing losses may need a signal-to-
noise ratio of as much as +30 dB to hear well. In a well-disciplined class-
room the background noise is likely to be at least 60 dB(A) and the teacher
would have to speak at around 90 dB(A) to achieve this. Personal or sound
field radio hearing aid systems (see sections 14.4.2 and 14.4.3) can help to
improve the signal-to-noise ratio and reduce strain on the teacher’s voice.
The signal-to-noise (S/N) ratio is the ratio between the signal level and
the level of the background noise. It is expressed in dB. A positive value
indicates that the signal is higher than the background noise. A negative
value indicates that it is lower than the background noise. An adult with
normal hearing can usually discriminate speech adequately in situations
where the signal-to-noise ratio is 0 dB and sometimes even less. However
to discriminate speech comfortably a signal-to-noise ratio of approxi-
mately +15 dB is needed. When hearing aids are being used, a higher
signal-to-noise ratio is required than with normal hearing. This is probably
due to distortion in the hearing aids (see section 8.3.5) and loss of infor-
mation due to hair cell loss in the cochlea (see section 2.2.2).
Classrooms in which deaf children are being taught should provide a
signal-to-noise ratio of at least +15 dB and a reverberation time no greater
than 0.4 seconds. This is often not the case. For example, in a recent

Paediatric provision 
307
survey of acoustic conditions in one particular secondary school (Maltby,
1999) the author found signal-to-noise ratios in normal classrooms were
between +5 dB and +10 dB but the most destructive feature of the school
acoustic environment was the high level of reverberation. Reverberation
times measured in normal classrooms varied from 0.77 seconds to
2.2 seconds (in a science laboratory). 
Reverberation is the reflection of sound. With normal hearing, rever-
beration is only a problem if the reverberation time is very long. The
ability to hear clearly is compromised if the direct signal and the reflected
signal arrive at the hearing aid microphone at different times (see section
1.1.7). Reverberation time can be measured as the time it takes for a
sound to cease reflecting and it can also be calculated mathematically
using the Sabine formula to provide a fairly accurate measure of RT60.
RT60 is the time taken for the sound level to drop by 60 dB.
Reverberation time is dependent upon the volume of the room and the
absorption of the materials within it. One sabin is equivalent to one
square foot of material with perfect absorption. This is given a coefficient
of 1. Absorption coefficients vary between 0, which is fully reflective, and
1, which is fully absorbent. The absorption is calculated as the sum of all
the separate frequency absorption values of each surface material within
the room. Reduction in reverberation time in the classroom may be
achieved by:
• Siting the classroom away from sources of noise.
• Careful use of acoustic materials. Absorbent materials tend to absorb
high frequencies to a much greater extent than low frequencies.
Acoustic treatment of the floor and back wall of the classroom is usually
very successful. Reflected sound from the ceiling and side walls gener-
ally have a positive rather than a negative effect.
• Good classroom management by the teacher.
• Appropriate hearing aid technology – for example, sound field amplifi-
cation. 
Degradation of the speech signal for the individual can be measured to
some extent using speech perception tests (see section 11.4.2).
Alternatively, Bruel and Kjaer’s Rapid Speech Transmission Index (RASTI)
can be used to measure the effect of the acoustic environment on a
speech-like signal. Perfect reception of the RASTI signal is given a score of
1.0. Leavitt and Flexer (1991) took RASTI measurements at 17 seating
positions in an occupied classroom. They demonstrated that a perfect
RASTI score of 1.0 could only be achieved at about 15 cm from the teacher.
The best seating position was the centre seat in the front row, which gave a

308
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
RASTI score of 0.83. In the back row, the RASTI score dropped to 0.55,
which is equivalent to a loss of 45% in speech intelligibility.
14.4.2 Radio hearing aid systems
Personal hearing aids depend for their effectiveness on being used close to
the speaker, in order to achieve a high signal-to-noise ratio. When the
speaker moves away the increasing distance quickly results in a weak
signal that is lost in the masking effect of background noise.
In schools, background noise and reverberation are often such as to
reduce the effectiveness of hearing aids significantly (Figure 14.8).
Figure 14.9. The basic format of a radio hearing aid.
Figure 14.8. As the distance away from the speaker increases, the intensity of the
signal falls (according to the inverse square law). Background noise then interferes
with the speech signal received by the hearing aid.
Radio frequency modulated (FM) systems are used to provide a short
microphone distance. The teacher wears the microphone and the pupil
wears a radio receiver, which is linked to a hearing aid. The signal is trans-
mitted to the pupil (Figure 14.9), regardless of his or her position, up to a
distance of about 100 m from the microphone. Radio hearing aids are used
at school and at home but their use is restricted by their expense and their
appearance, which is in most cases similar to a bodyworn hearing aid.

Paediatric provision 
309
There are two basic types of radio systems (Figure 14.10). Type 1 is a
complete radio hearing aid, in which the radio receiver is also a bodyworn
hearing aid. These tend to by used by some profoundly deaf children,
although they could equally well be used for children with mild hearing
losses who do not otherwise use a hearing aid. The main reason for not
doing so is the expense, as radio hearing aids are very costly.
Figure 14.10. The child’s unit in each of the types of radio system.
Type 2 is a system in which the transmitted signal is fed directly from
the radio receiver into the child’s individual hearing aid; this is known as
‘direct input’. This is the type most widely used because the child
continues to use their own hearing aid and an unchanged frequency
response. The radio receiver is usually linked directly into the personal
hearing aid via a lead and shoe (although there are also some radio
receivers that are housed in a large ‘shoe’ attached to the postaural aid –
they do not have a lead or bodyworn unit). 
In some cases the type 2 system with the bodyworn receiver is used with a
neck loop (instead of the connection via shoe and lead), which permits the
whole receiving unit to be hidden under clothing. However, the performance
of the induction loop tends to be inferior to that of direct input due to a
reduced low frequency response and an increased tendency to interference,
mainly from fluorescent lights and other electrical sources.
14.4.3 Sound field radio systems
A sound field system is basically a miniature public address system for the
classroom. Loudspeakers are positioned at various points to provide an
equal sound level throughout the room. The teacher uses a radio micro-
phone and transmitter, exactly as in the case of individual radio hearing
aids but pupils do not have to wear any equipment as they receive the

310
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
signal via the loudspeakers. The improved sound level in the classroom
can help all children to hear more clearly by improving the signal-to-noise
ratio. 
14.4.3 Educational provision
Many hearing-impaired children will need educational support from the
time of diagnosis throughout their education. Most hearing-impaired
children are educated within the ordinary or ‘mainstream’ school system,
with varying degrees of support. Ordinary schools provide an environ-
ment in which the child can gain experience of normal language and
behaviour, benefit from a wide curriculum, make friends among local
children and, in general, learn to adjust to being part of the hearing world.
For children with a significant hearing loss, support is usually provided in
the form of personal hearing aids, a radio system and the services of a
peripatetic or visiting teacher of the deaf. However, ordinary schools tend
to be large, noisy and often intolerant places and some children are
unable to cope. Hearing-impaired children may be placed in:
• normal ‘mainstream’ school;
• special class, or ‘unit’, within the ordinary school;
• special school.
Children who need significant extra support may be provided with a
‘statement of special educational needs’, under the 1981 Education Act. A
statement formalizes the child’s special needs and ways of meeting these,
while reinforcing the parent’s right to be involved in the process.
A special class or ‘partially hearing unit’ is an attempt to provide ‘the
best of both worlds’, with children integrated into ordinary classes as far as
possible but having an individual programme tailored to their needs and
with access to a full-time teacher of the deaf.
Special school provision has decreased as children are increasingly
placed within the normal school environment. Most of the special schools
that do remain cater for the needs of particular groups of severely and
profoundly deaf children, such as those who are:
• in need of a high level of support;
• of a particular religion;
• academically very able;
• using an alternative method of communication;
• multiply handicapped.

Paediatric provision 
311
Children in special schools have less access to ‘normal’ patterns of speech
and of behaviour and may be less prepared for coping with the demands
of everyday life but special schools can provide a concentration of
specialist staff and facilities. Children are educated within a protected
environment and in very small classes, consisting of not more than 10
children. Classrooms can be sound treated and specialist equipment often
includes group hearing aids and speech training units. A group hearing aid
is a hard-wired system in which each child wears headphones linked to a
desk unit capable of providing high output over an extended frequency
range (Figure 14.11) and, since the ear is not blocked, advantage is also
taken of ear canal resonance. The teacher uses a microphone that is also
wired into the system. Speech training equipment includes individual
hard-wired hearing aid units, often with a visual display to help the child
monitor his or her speech.
Approaches to the education of very severely and profoundly deaf
children vary markedly, especially between an ‘oral’ or ‘manual’ approach.
All systems make use of hearing aids and strive to develop language and
learning. Unfortunately, deep divisions exist between the supporters of
the various approaches, which is often unhelpful to parents because they
may receive conflicting advice. Most hearing-impaired children can learn
to communicate by speech if their loss is diagnosed early and treated
Figure 14.11. A group hearing aid in use at a school for deaf children. (Photograph
courtesy of St John’s School, Boston Spa.) The child on the right is wearing the
headphones of the group aid. The child on the left is using the system with a cochlear
implant.

312
Principles of Hearing Aid Audiology
promptly with effective hearing aids and skilled guidance. In essence,
‘oral’ methods seek to develop speech in a natural manner, while manual
or ‘total’ communication provides support to the spoken pattern in one of
a number of ways, namely using:
• sign language – this is often said to be the natural language of the deaf
community but unfortunately few hearing people are able to sign,
except in a slow and clumsy manner;
• finger-spelling – this can be likened to the written pattern and is of
limited use until the child has achieved some proficiency in language;
• cued speech – this is a method of communication in which hand
positions are used to supplement the information available through
lip-reading.
Special consideration must be given to the education of hearing-impaired
children. Hearing loss provides a starting point but is not a sufficient basis
on which to make a decision. The child’s whole development and his or
her family’s wishes should be considered in selecting an appropriate
school placement.
14.5 SUMMARY
Hearing impairment in childhood hinders development of the ability to
communicate and to make normal progress in school. Early diagnosis and
appropriate management are among the factors that can reduce the handi-
capping effect of hearing loss. Evaluation of hearing in early childhood
necessitates the use of special techniques appropriate to a child’s age and
stage of development. Educational placement should take full account of
the child’s needs and the parents’ wishes. Great emphasis is placed on the
full use of residual hearing through amplification in all educational
environments and specialist teachers of the deaf provide support. Most
hearing-impaired children are able to receive their education with the
normally hearing in ‘mainstream’ school, with the necessary specialist
support.

Glossary
A-scale The weighting network used with a sound level meter for free-
field sound measurements. The A-scale approximates to the inverted
40 phon equal loudness curve.
Acoustic feedback The excitation of the microphone by sound gener-
ated from the receiver of the same system.
Acoustic neuroma Benign eighth nerve tumour.
Acoustic ohm Unit of acoustic impedance.
Acoustic reflex Contraction of middle ear muscles in response to loud
sound.
Acoustic trauma Noise damage due to sudden impulse noise of high
intensity.
Acoustics Study of science of sound.
Action potential Brief electrical discharge that occurs when a nerve cell
is activated.
Acute Short and severe, (of 21 days or less duration).
Adaptation (auditory) Reduction in hearing sensitivity within a brief
time of the sound onset.
Adhesion New and abnormal tissue growth due to inflammation.
Admittance The ease with which sound waves pass through.
Air–bone gap The difference in dB between the air conduction reading
on an audiogram and the bone conduction reading for the same ear. If
10 dB or greater indicates a conductive element.
Algorithm  A set of rules.
Alport’s syndrome Kidney disorder combined with hearing and sight
defects.
Ambient Surrounding or background.
Amplitude The maximum displacement of any wave from its equilibrium
position.
Analgesic Pain-killing drug, for example aspirin.
Anechoic With no echoes or significant sound reflection: ‘dead’.
ANSI American National Standards Institute.

315
Anoxia Lack of oxygen.
Aphasia Disorder of central nervous system causing difficulty with speech
and language.
Artificial ear
A 6 cc coupling device used in audiometer earphone
calibration.
Artificial mastoid Calibration device for bone vibrators, which simulates
the mechanical impedance of an average normal human mastoid.
Atresia Absence of an opening.
Atrophy  Wasting away due to cell degeneration.
Attenuation Reduction.
Audiogram A graph showing a person’s hearing thresholds for pure
tones.
Audiometry The measurement of hearing.
Auditory filters Overlapping band-pass filters representing filtering in
the cochlea.
Auditory training Teaching a hearing impaired person to make best use
of residual hearing.
Aural Of the ear.
Aural–oral approach A method of teaching hearing impaired children

Auricle Pinna.
Auropalpebral reflex (APR) Eyeblink in response to loud sound.
Automatic gain control (AGC) Automatic reduction in gain of a hearing
aid. Compression.
Azimuth Distance in degrees, for example 0° azimuth = straight ahead;
90° azimuth = to person’s right.
Barotrauma Fluid in the middle ear due to sudden pressure change.
Behavioural audiometry
Assessment of hearing by noting bodily
responses.
Békésy audiometry Automatic audiometry.
Benign Not cancerous.
Binaural summation Improvement in hearing threshold when hearing
via both ears. Usually at least 3 dB and may be much more.
Brief tone Tone of 500 ms or less.
BSI British Standards Institution.
Calibration
Checking, and adjusting if necessary, the output of a
measurement device, such as an audiometer.
Capillaries Small blood vessels.
Central In brain and/or spinal cord.
Cholesteatoma Cyst-like growth in the middle ear containing shed skin.

Chronic Having lasted a long time (over 8 weeks).
Click A short impulse of sound, too short in duration for its characteris-
tics to be fully perceived.
CMV Cytomegalovirus disease.
Cochlear microphonic
Electrical output from cochlear hair cells,
following input signal.
Compliance Opposite of stiffness; ability to give.
Compression (1) Limiting of maximum output, using automatic gain
control to reduce the gain beyond a predetermined intensity level. (See
also compression limiting and dynamic range compression.) 
(2) Air at more than atmospheric pressure (as in the compressions of a
sound wave).
Compression limiting A method of limiting output with low distortion.
Compression limiting can be input or output compression but will have
a high kneepoint and a high compression ratio in order to avoid satura-
tion whilst maintaining linear amplification for as long as possible.
Conditioning Rewarding of required behaviour – used widely in assess-
ment of children.
Congenital Present at birth.
Contralateral On the opposite side.
Critical band The narrow band of noise required to mask a pure-tone
signal.
Cross-hearing Sound presented to one ear is perceived in the other by
vibration of the skull.
Curvilinear compression Compression with a compression ratio that
increases as the input increases.
Derecruitment Abnormally slow growth of loudness, usually due to
disorder of auditory nerve.
Dichotic Affecting each ear differently.
Difference limen (for example, of intensity or frequency)
The
smallest detectable difference between two signals.
Digital hearing aid
A hearing aid in which the electrical signal is
converted to digital values for modification before being converted
back to an electrical signal which is passed to the receiver.
Diotic Identical signal to each ear.
Diplacusis One pure tone appears to the listener as two or more tones.
Directional microphone One that is more responsive to sound from one
direction – normally the front.
Distortion Any unwanted sound present in the output that was not
present in the input.
Diuretic Drug to increase urination.

Glossary 
317
Down’s syndrome (also called Down syndrome) A congenital abnor-
mality, formerly known as mongolism. Features include mental retarda-
tion, mongoloid eyes, flattened facial features, short, broad hands and
feet, small  pinnae, narrow ear canals and a high incidence of conductive
and sensorineural hearing loss.
Dynamic range The difference in dB between the threshold of hearing
and the threshold of discomfort.
Dynamic range compression Compression that allows for the client’s
reduced dynamic range by amplifying low intensity input more than 
high intensity input and keeps output below uncomfortable loudness
level. 
Earhook That part of a postaural aid that fits over the ear – also called the
elbow.
Earphone A loudspeaker at the ear.
Ear simulator A coupler that simulates the acoustic characteristics of an
average adult ear.
Effective gain The difference between aided and unaided thresholds.
Effusion An abnormal outpouring of fluid.
Electro-acoustic device A device that converts electrical to acoustic
signals and vice versa.
Electrocochleography Recording electrical signals (action potentials)
from the eighth nerve.
Environmental microphone
A microphone that picks up airborne
sound – for example, an environmental microphone may be built into a
radio hearing aid so the pupils can hear themselves and their
colleagues as well as the teacher.
Fall time The time needed for a signal to decay by 60 dB from its steady
state.
Feedback (acoustic) The whistling that occurs from a hearing aid when
sound leaks from the receiver and is picked up and reamplified via the
microphone.
Feedback Information that is used to maintain or modify performance.
Filter (acoustic) Allows some sounds to pass but attenuates others.
Formant A special pattern of speech sounds, determined by the vocal
cords and the resonant cavities of the vocal tract, giving each vowel its
recognizable quality.
Fossa Groove.
Free-field Open space – or an area with boundaries that are negligible for
the test.
Frequency analyser A sound level meter with an octave band (or other)

filter. Displays amplitude versus frequency on graph of input.
Fulcrum Pivot.
Functional gain Real ear gain.
Functional hearing Non-organic or psychogenic loss.
Fundamental frequency The lowest frequency in a periodic waveform.
Harmonic A frequency that is a whole number multiple of the pure tone
to which it is related.
Haematoma Blood clot due to a break in a blood vessel.
Hertz A unit used in the measurement of frequency of sound, equivalent
to the number of cycles per second.
High compression ratio
One that is 5:1 or more – having greater
compression. 
High-pass filter Reduces low frequencies while allowing high frequen-
cies to pass unaffected.
Homeostasis Equilibrium – achieving a normal balance.
Horn A tapered sound tube.

Immitance Impedance or admittance.
Impedance (acoustic) Resistance to the passage of sound.

Incidence The number of cases in a given population (for example, as a
number of live births).
Incident wave Any wave that has its path interrupted by an object; the
wave is then said to be incident on this object.
Induction coil Picks up electromagnetic signals from a loop amplifier;
activated in a hearing aid by the ‘T’ switch.
Inflammation Sore, swollen, red area. The body’s response to injury or
infection.
Insertion gain The difference between the gain of the open ear and the
gain of the ear wearing a hearing aid: aided – unaided real ear gain.
Insertion loss The difference between the gain of the open ear and that
of an occluded ear.
In situ In the natural position.
Intelligibility The degree to which speech can be understood.
Interaural attenuation The degree to which sound is reduced as it
crosses the head. Minimum interaural attenuation is 40 dB for air
conducted signals  and 0 dB for  bone conduction.
Intermodulation Frequencies in the output that are the sum and the
difference of two or more input frequencies and their harmonics.

Masking Raising of the threshold of hearing for one sound by the intro-

Maximum power
Saturation or output sound pressure level. The

Ménière’s disorder
Idiopathic episodic endolymphatic hydrops. A

Most comfortable level (MCL)  There is an area, within the residual

That part of the normal dynamic range that the

Saturation
Maximum – any further increase in input will cause no
increase in output.
Sensation level Amount in dB above the individual’s threshold.
Sensorineural Impairment caused in or beyond the cochlea.
Sequelae After effects of disease.
Sibilant High frequency consonant of a hissing nature, for example ‘s’ as
in sue, ‘S’ (or ‘sh’) as in shoe.
Signal to noise ratio Signal level compared to the competing noise.
Usually expressed as signal minus noise, for example, a signal of
60 dBA with a competing noise level of 50 dBA gives S/N of + 10 dB.
Signs Signs of a disorder are whatever features are noted by the doctor,

Sintered mesh A filter made of partially fused metal.
Sound field Containing sound waves, for example in a room.
Sound level meter A device to measure sound pressure in dB.

Spectrograph A three-dimensional display of the energy of speech.
Frequency and time are represented by the two axes of the graph.
Intensity is shown by the darkness of the trace.
Speculum  Examination tip used with an otoscope.
Speech spectrum The average level of the speech sounds.
Speech reception threshold The faintest level at which a person can
identify 50% in a speech test.
Speech reading Lip-reading – but includes use of all visual cues not just
those visible on the lips.
